  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_07ed9d65-b6a5-4d20-bed2-2619eccd53df'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13304704903594098889">Jacqueline Woodson, one of today's finest writers, tells the moving story of her childhood in mesmerizing verse.<br/><br/>Raised in South Carolina and New York, Woodson always felt halfway home in each place. In vivid poems, she shares what it was like to grow up as an African American in the 1960s and 1970s, living with the remnants of Jim Crow and her growing awareness of the Civi</span>
  <span id="freeText13304704903594098889" style="display:none">Jacqueline Woodson, one of today's finest writers, tells the moving story of her childhood in mesmerizing verse.<br /><br />Raised in South Carolina and New York, Woodson always felt halfway home in each place. In vivid poems, she shares what it was like to grow up as an African American in the 1960s and 1970s, living with the remnants of Jim Crow and her growing awareness of the Civil Rights movement. Touching and powerful, each poem is both accessible and emotionally charged, each line a glimpse into a child’s soul as she searches for her place in the world. Woodson’s eloquent poetry also reflects the joy of finding her voice through writing stories, despite the fact that she struggled with reading as a child. Her love of stories inspired her and stayed with her, creating the first sparks of the gifted writer she was to become.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13304704903594098889" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        $('edition_29093222').observe('prototip:shown', function () {

          });

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/311133-why-are-people-saying-this-is">why are people saying this is middle-reader? sure its consumable at that level but that doesn&#39;t mean it&#39;s not an adult book with complexities and plenty of layers (and meter) ... it&#39;s just much more readable than a lot of books the critics try to shove down our throats as literary.</a>
</div>

<div id="review_1768238121" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1768238121" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1768238121?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=african-american">african-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=middle-grade-kids">middle-grade-kids</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1768238121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15345913514945004151">I recently read Jacqueline Woodson's Another Brooklyn, and people here recommended that I read her middle grade kids book Brown Girl Dreaming. Like Another Brooklyn, Brown Girl Dreaming is a poetic account of Woodson's upbringing in South Carolina and Brooklyn. The entire book flows in dreamy poetry as Woodson describes growing up during the 1960s, and for that I rate it 4 lovely stars. <br/><br/>Jacqueline Woodson was born to Jack Woodson and Mary Ann Irby in 1963 in Columbus, Ohio. Her father was determ</span>
  <span id="freeText15345913514945004151" style="display:none">I recently read Jacqueline Woodson's Another Brooklyn, and people here recommended that I read her middle grade kids book Brown Girl Dreaming. Like Another Brooklyn, Brown Girl Dreaming is a poetic account of Woodson's upbringing in South Carolina and Brooklyn. The entire book flows in dreamy poetry as Woodson describes growing up during the 1960s, and for that I rate it 4 lovely stars. <br /><br />Jacqueline Woodson was born to Jack Woodson and Mary Ann Irby in 1963 in Columbus, Ohio. Her father was determined that his children not live a life enmeshed in Jim Crow so he desired to live in the north. Yet, Mary Ann pined for her Greenville, South Carolina home, and this soon become a source of friction. The couple divorced, and the Irbys returned to South Carolina when Jacqueline was just a baby. <br /><br />Mary Ann longed to join her siblings Kay and Robert in Brooklyn so for long stretches she left her children with her parents Gunnar and Georgia in Greenville. Jacqueline writes of sipping hot chocolate on the porch, fireflies at dusk, and curling up with her siblings in her grandparents' bed. Despite the lingering de facto segregation, Greenville appears to be a wholesome place to raise a family. Even when the Irbys arrived in Brooklyn to stay, Jacqueline and her older siblings spent the summers in Greenville as their mother worked a full time job. Her writing reflects her dual homes- Brooklyn and Greenville, and her loyalty to both places. <br /><br />In Bushwick, Jacqueline describes what it was like to be a kid in the late 1960s. At PS 106, she had teachers who saw the writer in her and encouraged her to share her gift with others. She also meets her forever friend Maria who moved in next door, and the girls become as close as family, even though their cultures are vastly different. Bushwick also appears to be a safe place to grow up. The Black Panthers at the time were hinted at as being in California, the melting pot of cultures in Brooklyn appear here to thrive in concert with one another. Jacqueline enjoys Maria's mother's arroz con frijoles yet also excels at double Dutch. While describing the friction in the world at large, Woodson still writes in flowery poetry that remains dreamlike in prose. <br /><br />It is oftentimes difficult for me to find quality middle grade books for my children. I have read several books this year from recommendations from Goodreads friends. It is a relief to know there are quality books for my kids that are also enjoyable for adults. Jacqueline Woodson has been writing since childhood and has been published for over twenty years and won multiple awards for her work. Brown Girl Dreaming won a National Book Award in 2014, and it is a book that I would easily allow my children to read. An amazing poet and writer, I look forward to reading more of Woodson's books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15345913514945004151" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2068867954" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2068867954" />
    <a title="Candace" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16384209-candace"><img alt="Candace" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459720769p2/16384209.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2068867954?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Candace" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Candace" href="/user/show/16384209-candace">Candace</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22909597-brown-girl-dreaming">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16384209-candace?shelf=listened-to-audio-version">listened-to-audio-version</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16384209-candace?shelf=read-listened-with-my-kids">read-listened-with-my-kids</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2068867954" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10338239620560623827">I listened to this audiobook with my two daughters (1st grade and 5th grade) and my grandmother on our most recent road trip. This book is beautifully written and the imagery was spectacular. It managed to captivate everyone in the car, which is saying something since there were 3 distinct generations represented.<br/><br/>Jacqueline Wilson describes her childhood, growing up in the 1960's. In her youth, her time was divided amongst Ohio, South Carolina, and later, New York. Each area provided a different</span>
  <span id="freeText10338239620560623827" style="display:none">I listened to this audiobook with my two daughters (1st grade and 5th grade) and my grandmother on our most recent road trip. This book is beautifully written and the imagery was spectacular. It managed to captivate everyone in the car, which is saying something since there were 3 distinct generations represented.<br /><br />Jacqueline Wilson describes her childhood, growing up in the 1960's. In her youth, her time was divided amongst Ohio, South Carolina, and later, New York. Each area provided a different experience and a vastly different culture. This gave Ms. Woodson the unique ability to describe her experiences, particularly those related to the civil rights movement, across a variety of social settings and geographic locales.<br /><br />As we were driving, I had to pause this story several times to answer questions and explain things to my daughters. My oldest daughter, at ten years-old, had a lot of questions. This initiated some important discussions and proved to be quite enlightening for all parties.<br /><br />It never ceases to amaze me how children can spot "right" and "wrong" so clearly, before external forces try to taint their inner goodness. Trying to explain the existence of Jim Crow laws and why Rosa Park's decision to sit at the front of the bus sparked such controversy at the time, led to some very interesting discussions. Sometimes, adults can learn from children and should follow their lead. The hatefulness and wrongness of those discriminatory and racist laws were incredibly obvious to my children. The fact that this was ever allowed to go on was very difficult for their minds to grasp. <br /><br />Overall, this was a very moving and thought-provoking read. It is the type of book that encourages insightful discussions and instills strong values at a young age. It introduces young readers to some difficult, but important topics and raises awareness, lest we repeat history. I would definitely recommend this book to others.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10338239620560623827" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1910601686" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1910601686" />
    <a title="Hannah Greendale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43377498-hannah-greendale"><img alt="Hannah Greendale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466436186p2/43377498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1910601686?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah Greendale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah Greendale" href="/user/show/43377498-hannah-greendale">Hannah Greendale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=poetry-or-verse">poetry-or-verse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=national-book-award-nominee">national-book-award-nominee</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=literary">literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=newbery-honor">newbery-honor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=national-book-award-winner">national-book-award-winner</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1910601686" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5738952618616279107"><a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sRNrlCCWpVk' rel='nofollow noopener'>Click here</a> to watch a video review of this book on my channel, <i>From Beginning to Bookend</i>. <br/><br/>Jacqeuline Woodson recalls what life was like growing up in the 1960s and 1970s in this autobiographical middle grade novel. Written in verse, her account portrays a life divided between the North and the South, learning about the civil rights movement, and discovering a burgeoning passion for writing stories. <br/><br/><i>I am born as the South explodes, <br/>too many people too many years <br/>enslaved, then emancipated<br/>but not </i></span>
  <span id="freeText5738952618616279107" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sRNrlCCWpVk" rel="nofollow noopener">Click here</a> to watch a video review of this book on my channel, <i>From Beginning to Bookend</i>. <br /><br />Jacqeuline Woodson recalls what life was like growing up in the 1960s and 1970s in this autobiographical middle grade novel. Written in verse, her account portrays a life divided between the North and the South, learning about the civil rights movement, and discovering a burgeoning passion for writing stories. <br /><br /><i>I am born as the South explodes, <br />too many people too many years <br />enslaved, then emancipated<br />but not free, the people <br />who look like me<br />keep fighting<br />and marching<br />and getting killed<br />so that today - <br />February 12, 1963<br />and every day from this moment on, <br />brown children like me can grow up <br />free. Can grow up <br />learning and voting and walking and riding<br />wherever</i> we<i> want.</i> <br /><br />Woodson introduces young readers to important figures, such as Martin Luther King Jr., Malcolm X, Rosa Parks, and author James Baldwin. <br /><br /><i>Not even three years have passed since a brown girl <br />named Ruby Bridges<br />walked into an all-white school. <br />Armed guards surrounded her while hundreds of white people spat and called her names. <br /><br />She was six years old.</i> <br /><br />Whether she was living in Brooklyn, New York or spending the summer with her grandparents in South Carolina, Woodson never felt she fit in but, no matter where she was, family was her anchor. Family was her shelter. The ways in which she portrays the sense of love and security found in even the simplest aspects of being with family - such as enjoying a warm meal - are sublime. <br /><br /><i>Autumn is coming. <br />Outside, there's the sound of wind<br />through the pine trees. <br />But inside there are stories, there are biscuits<br />and grits and eggs, the fire in the potbellied stove<br />already filling the house with warmth.</i> <br /><br />She also paints a vivid portrayal of growing up at a time when race relations were strained to the point of breaking and learning to fight for one's rights and protect oneself in a violent society was as common as learning how to tie one's shoes. <br /><br /><i>This is the way brown people have to fight,</i> <br />my grandfather says. <br /><i>You can't just put your fist up. You have to insist <br />on something<br />gently. Walk toward a thing<br />slowly.</i> <br /><br /><i>They learn <br />how to change the South without violence, <br />how not to be moved<br />by the evil actions of others, how to walk slowly but<br />with deliberate steps. <br />How to sit at counters and be cursed at<br />without cursing back, have food and drink poured<br />over them without standing up and hurting someone. <br />Even the teenagers<br />get trained to sit tall, not cry, swallow back fear.</i> <br /><br /><i>Brown Girl Dreaming</i> is a cadent portrait of a little girl cherishing her family and dreaming of a better world. <br /><br /><i>We all have the same dream,</i> my grandmother says.<i><br />To live equal in a country that's supposed to be<br />the land of the free.</i> <br />She lets out a long breath, <br />deep remembering.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5738952618616279107" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6552111595560138221"><br/>I've never read anything quite like this before - the telling of a life in such a unique way. The writing is lovely , the way it is told - memories in free style poetry. I don't know what I can say to do justice to what Woodson has accomplished here. Is it a memoir, a novel, a book of poetry? No matter how it is categorized, it is clear that this is precisely what Woodson says about it in her author's note "And that's what this book is - my past, my people, my memories, my story."<br/><br/>This is perhaps</span>
  <span id="freeText6552111595560138221" style="display:none"><br />I've never read anything quite like this before - the telling of a life in such a unique way. The writing is lovely , the way it is told - memories in free style poetry. I don't know what I can say to do justice to what Woodson has accomplished here. Is it a memoir, a novel, a book of poetry? No matter how it is categorized, it is clear that this is precisely what Woodson says about it in her author's note "And that's what this book is - my past, my people, my memories, my story."<br /><br />This is perhaps aimed at a YA audience as most of her books are but I didn't view it that way. I think anyone of any age can appreciate this story of her family, the places she lived, the times in which she grew up, how her writing life developed and how she followed her dream . I can't say enough about the beautiful writing. She has provided us a tremendous sense of time and place growing up in the 1960's and 1970's in Greenville, SC and Brooklyn, NY. and the inner thoughts of a young girl who dreams of becoming a writer. <br /><br />Just before this I read a copy of Woodson's new book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27213163.Another_Brooklyn" title="Another Brooklyn by Jacqueline Woodson" rel="nofollow noopener">Another Brooklyn</a>, to be published on 8/9/16 and I'll say the same thing here as I did in my review of it , she was born to write and I am grateful for profound experience it was for me to see her journey. </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1759195385556856758">brown girl dreaming, tu es formidable. <br/><br/>The more I look at this poetic memoir, the more I fall in love. These short poems have imprinted themselves onto my heart. They long to be remembered. And they will be, because they are powerful and true and incomparable.<br/><br/>brown girl dreaming, tu es inoubliable. <br/><br/>Jacqueline Woodson grew up in the North, with her mother, her father and her sister, and then she grew up in the South, with her mother, her sister, her brother and her grandparents, after her mother </span>
  <span id="freeText1759195385556856758" style="display:none">brown girl dreaming, tu es formidable. <br /><br />The more I look at this poetic memoir, the more I fall in love. These short poems have imprinted themselves onto my heart. They long to be remembered. And they will be, because they are powerful and true and incomparable.<br /><br />brown girl dreaming, tu es inoubliable. <br /><br />Jacqueline Woodson grew up in the North, with her mother, her father and her sister, and then she grew up in the South, with her mother, her sister, her brother and her grandparents, after her mother left her father. Her grandpa became her new Daddy. Later, she also grew up in the in-between North and South, New York. <br /><br />brown girl dreaming, tu es une illumination.<br /><br />Jackie wanted to read and write and dream and play, and the people around her wanted change and equality and marches. Her family sat at the back when they took the bus—they had to behave, they were oppressed, like all black people in and before the 60s. Yes, things have changed since the 19th century—slavery, for instance, was abolished—but not nearly enough.<br /><br />brown girl dreaming, tu es une merveille. <br /><br />By broaching such important topics, like segregation, equal treatment, movement, racism and oppression, this beautiful and moving memoir makes the reader think seriously about what it means to be human, what it means to be tolerated, accepted and loved. It’s important we don’t forget about the past, because we have to do everything in our means not to repeat it.<br /><br />brown girl dreaming, tu es vraie. <br /><br /><a href="https://hitormissbooks.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/c/LolaReviewer" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a> | <a>Twitter</a> | <a>Instagram</a> | <a href="https://plus.google.com/u/0/105021852309973899120/posts" rel="nofollow noopener">Google+</a> | <a href="https://www.bloglovin.com/blogs/hit-miss-books-12564715" rel="nofollow noopener">Bloglovin’</a></span>

<div id="review_1129370027" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1129370027" />
    <a title="Julie Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine"><img alt="Julie Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445900882p2/1213607.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1129370027?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Christine" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine">Julie Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=bio-autobio-memoir">bio-autobio-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=read-2015">read-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=usa-contemporary">usa-contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=best-of-2015">best-of-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1129370027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11957393626726789952">I’m having the most difficult time writing a review for <i>brown girl dreaming</i>. It’s so hard to bubble over and breathe and cry and write, all at the same time. Each and every page is a gift of wisdom and innocence and discovery. Heartbreak. Joy. Family. Loneliness. Childhood. History. I savored and smiled as I read. I wept. I rushed out to buy my own copy. I wish I could buy enough copies for the world. <br/><br/>My only reading goal for 2015 is to read more poetry. Without design—just luck of the queue at </span>
  <span id="freeText11957393626726789952" style="display:none">I’m having the most difficult time writing a review for <i>brown girl dreaming</i>. It’s so hard to bubble over and breathe and cry and write, all at the same time. Each and every page is a gift of wisdom and innocence and discovery. Heartbreak. Joy. Family. Loneliness. Childhood. History. I savored and smiled as I read. I wept. I rushed out to buy my own copy. I wish I could buy enough copies for the world. <br /><br />My only reading goal for 2015 is to read more poetry. Without design—just luck of the queue at the library—<i>brown girl dreaming</i>, a memoir in verse, was the first book I completed this year. There is something sublime in that serendipity. <br />The book’s opening poem signals the story Jacqueline Woodson seeks to tell:<br /><blockquote>I am born on a Tuesday at University Hospital<br />Columbus, Ohio,<br />USA—<br />A country caught<br /><br />Between Black and White.</blockquote><br />Woodson reminds us that when she was born in 1963, “...only seven years had passed since Rose Parks refused to give up her seat on a city bus” in Montgomery, Alabama. The author, too, is of the South, but also of the Midwest and of the North. She moved with her mother, sister and brother to Greenville, South Carolina—to her mother’s family—when she was a toddler, and then to Brooklyn, New York in elementary school. <br /><br /><i>brown girl dreaming</i> is also the story of a little girl finding her voice. In Woodson’s case, it was the discovery that words and stories belonged to her—she just needed the time to meet them on her own terms: <br /><br /><blockquote>I am not my sister. <br />Words from the books curl around each other<br />make little sense<br />until<br />I read them again<br />and again, the story<br />settling into memory. <i>Too slow</i> my teacher says.<br /><i>Read Faster. </i><br /><i>Too babyish</i>, the teacher says.<br /><i>Read older. </i><br />But I don't want to read faster or older or<br />any way else that might<br />make the story disappear too quickly from where<br />it's settling<br />inside my brain,<br />slowly becoming a part of me. <br />A story I will remember <br />long after I've read it for the second, third, <br />tenth, hundredth time.</blockquote><br />There is such joy and love in her verse, a profound appreciation for her family and for the places that make up her visions of home. She writes of her mother’s parents in South Carolina: <br /><blockquote>So the first time my mother goes to New York City<br />we don’t know to be sad, the weight<br />of our grandparents’ love like a blanket<br />with us beneath it,<br />safe and warm. </blockquote><br />And of Brooklyn:<br /><blockquote>We take our food out to her stoop just as the grown-ups<br />start dancing merengue, the women lifting their long dresses<br />to show off their fast-moving feet,<br />the men clapping and yelling,<br />Baila! Baila! until the living room floor disappears. </blockquote><br />You may find <i>brown girl dreaming</i> on the fiction shelves of bookstores and libraries, for it is classified as a “fictionalized memoir.” Leaving aside debates of genre, it is far more likely to find a readership from these fiction shelves, and that is a good and necessary thing. <i>Memoir</i> and <i>free verse</i> may seem like odd companions, particularly in a book meant for younger readers, but oh, what a stellar opportunity to read and teach the power of poetry. <br /><br /><i>brown girl dreaming</i> received the 2014 National Book Award for Young People’s Literature and is ostensibly a book meant for middle-grade readers, but it is timeless in its grace and eloquence. I recommend it to everyone, regardless of age. <br /><br />Were I a pre-teen, I know I’d be reading this at every available moment: at the breakfast table, on the bus, in the cafeteria, in my room instead of suffering through long division homework and answering questions on the Emancipation Proclamation at the end of chapter 27 in my Social Studies text. The intimacy and immediacy of <i>brown girl dreaming</i> feels like a secret passed between BFFs, a Technicolor “now” of an After-School Special, the story of an American kid my age that is at once familiar in emotion and exotic in setting. <br /><br />Were I the parent of a pre-teen or a younger child, we would read this together, for this is the history of America in the 1960s, and it offers so many of those “teachable moments”: opportunities to reach for history books, to seek out primary sources, to watch videos of speeches and documentaries of a time that is both distant, yet still very much at hand. The same would hold true for a book club of adults. <i>brown girl dreaming</i> can serve as a touchstone for African-American literature and history, which is <i>our shared</i> history.<br /><br />As an adult, I read this with humility and wonder, enchanted by the voice of young Jacqueline Woodson as she discovers the importance of place, self, family, and words. As a writer, I am awed and overjoyed by the beauty of her language, by the richness of her verse. <br /><br /><blockquote>Even the silence <br />has a story to tell you. <br />Just listen. Listen.</blockquote> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11957393626726789952" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1811725754?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nat" href="/user/show/23381142-nat">Nat</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1811725754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer815766696618765889">Told through vivid poems, Jacqueline Woodson shares what it was like to grow up as an African American in the 1960s and 1970s. She covers everything from race to religion to the Civil Rights movement. Woodson's life was very complicated and very rich in detail, which I really loved. She's a natural storyteller that made me feel like I was transported back to each event through her writing. And I will remember her story for a long time to come.<br/><br/>As a result, I decided to share some of my favorite p</span>
  <span id="freeText815766696618765889" style="display:none">Told through vivid poems, Jacqueline Woodson shares what it was like to grow up as an African American in the 1960s and 1970s. She covers everything from race to religion to the Civil Rights movement. Woodson's life was very complicated and very rich in detail, which I really loved. She's a natural storyteller that made me feel like I was transported back to each event through her writing. And I will remember her story for a long time to come.<br><br>As a result, I decided to share some of my favorite passages that were featured in here, so that I can come back to this review and reminisce:<br><br><b>daywork</b><br><br><i>“I’m not ashamed</i>, she says,<br><i>cleaning is what I know. I’m not ashamed,<br>if it feeds my children.”<br><br>“Don’t any of you ever do daywork</i>, she warns us.<br><i>I’m doing it now so you don’t have to.”</i><br><br><b>the reader</b><br><br><i>“When we can’t find my sister, we know<br>she is under the kitchen table, a book in her hand,<br>a glass of milk and a small bowl of peanuts beside her.<br><br>We know we can call Odella’s name out loud,<br>slap the table hard with our hands,<br>dance around it singing<br>“She’ll Be Coming ’Round the Mountain”<br>so many times the song makes us sick<br>and the circling makes us dizzy<br>and still<br>my sister will do nothing more<br>than slowly turn the page.”</i><br><br><b>south carolina at war</b><br><br><i>“This is the way brown people have to fight,</i><br>my grandfather says.<br><i>You can’t just put your fist up. You have to insist<br>on something<br>gently. Walk toward a thing<br>slowly.<br><br>But be ready to die,</i><br>my grandfather says,<br><i>for what is right.<br><br>Be ready to die</i>, my grandfather says,<br><i>for everything you believe in.”</i><br><br><i>“More than a hundred years</i>, my grandfather says,<br><i>and we’re still fighting for the free life<br>we’re supposed to be living.”</i><br><br><b>the stories cora tells</b><br><br><i>“Don’t believe everything you hear, Jackie.<br>Someday, you’ll come to know<br>when someone is telling the truth<br>and when they’re just making up stories.”</i><br><br><b>composition notebook</b><br><br><i>“Nothing in the world is like this—<br>a bright white page with<br>pale blue lines. The smell of a newly sharpened pencil<br>the soft hush of it<br>moving finally<br>one day<br>into letters.”</i><br><br><b>writing #1</b><br><br><i>“It’s easier to make up stories<br>than it is to write them down. When I speak,<br>the words come pouring out of me. The story<br>wakes up and walks all over the room. Sits in a chair,<br>crosses one leg over the other, says,<br>Let me introduce myself. Then just starts going on and on.<br>But as I bend over my composition notebook,<br>only my name<br>comes quickly. Each letter, neatly printed<br>between the pale blue lines. Then white<br>space and air and me wondering, How do I<br>spell introduce? Trying again and again<br>until there is nothing but pink<br>bits of eraser and a hole now<br>where a story should be.”</i><br><br><b>the other woodson</b><br><br><i>“Even though so many people think my sister and I<br>are twins,<br>I am the other Woodson, following behind her each year<br>into the same classroom she had the year before. Each<br>teacher smiles when they call my name. Woodson, they<br>say. You must be Odella’s sister. Then they nod<br>slowly, over and over again, call me Odella. Say,<br>I’m sorry! You look so much like her and she is SO brilliant!<br>then wait for my brilliance to light up<br>the classroom. Wait for my arm to fly into<br>the air with every answer. Wait for my pencil<br>to move quickly through the too-easy math problems<br>on the mimeographed sheet. Wait for me to stand<br>before class, easily reading words even high school<br>students stumble over. And they keep waiting.<br>And waiting<br>and waiting<br>and waiting<br><br>until one day, they walk into the classroom,<br>almost call me Odel—then stop<br>remember that I am the other Woodson<br><br>and begin searching for brilliance<br><br>at another desk.”</i><br><br><b>reading</b><br><br><i>“I am not my sister.<br>Words from the books curl around each other<br>make little sense<br>until<br>I read them again<br>and again, the story<br>settling into memory. Too slow<br>the teacher says.<br>Read faster.<br>Too babyish, the teacher says.<br>Read older.<br>But I don’t want to read faster or older or<br>any way else that might<br>make the story disappear too quickly from where <br>it’s settling<br>inside my brain,<br>slowly becoming<br>a part of me.<br>A story I will remember<br>long after I’ve read it for the second, third,<br>tenth, hundredth time.”</i><br><br>That last sentence is so beautiful!!<br><br><b>stevie and me</b><br><br><i>“Every Monday, my mother takes us<br>to the library around the corner. We are allowed<br>to take out seven books each. On those days,<br>no one complains<br>that all I want are picture books.<br><br>Those days, no one tells me to read faster<br>to read harder books<br>to read like Dell.<br><br>No one is there to say, Not that book,<br>when I stop in front of the small paperback<br>with a brown boy on the cover.<br>Stevie.<br><br>I read:<br>One day my momma told me,<br>“You know you’re gonna have<br>a little friend come stay with you.”<br>And I said, “Who is it?”<br><br>If someone had been fussing with me<br>to read like my sister, I might have missed<br>the picture book filled with brown people, more<br>brown people than I’d ever seen<br>in a book before.<br><br>The little boy’s name was Steven but<br>his mother kept calling him Stevie.<br>My name is Robert but my momma don’t<br>call me Robertie.<br><br>If someone had taken<br>that book out of my hand<br>said, You’re too old for this<br>maybe<br>I’d never have believed<br>that someone who looked like me<br>could be in the pages of the book<br>that someone who looked like me<br>had a story.”</i><br><br>REPRESENTATION MATTERS!!!<br><br><b>how to listen #7</b><br><br><i>“Even the silence<br>has a story to tell you.<br>Just listen. Listen.”</i><br><a href="https://bookspoils.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1472201424i/20164163.png" width="500" height="40" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>To say <b>Brown Girl Dreaming</b> hit home would be an understatement. This book came at the exact right moment for me to read. And the way Woodson told her story and that of her family felt like I was right there.<br><br>To quote the author:<br><br><i>“As my sister reads, the pictures begin forming<br>as though someone has turned on a television,<br>lowered the sound,<br>pulled it up close.<br>Grainy black-and-white pictures come slowly at me<br>Deep. Infinite. Remembered”</i><br><br>Speaking of, the writing in here was gorgeously elucidated. I'm craving more of Jacqueline Woodson's words, so I'm hoping to pick up <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/129771.The_Other_Side" rel="nofollow noopener">The Other Side</a> in the near future.<br><br>Oh, and I also really appreciated that the author took the time to include some great family photos:<br><br><a href="https://bookspoils.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479725354i/21227004.png" width="533" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><a href="https://bookspoils.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479725354i/21227005.png" width="533" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><a href="https://bookspoils.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479725354i/21227006.png" width="533" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><a href="https://bookspoils.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479725354i/21227007.png" width="533" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><i>*Note: I'm an Amazon Affiliate. If you're interested in buying</i> <b>Brown Girl Dreaming</b>, <i>just click on the image below to go through my link. I'll make a small commission!*</i><br><a target="_blank" href="https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0399252517/ref=as_li_tl?ie=UTF8&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=9325&amp;creativeASIN=0399252517&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;tag=natalie013-20&amp;linkId=128cbbfefe8e73c8ef929392b0ae8988" rel="nofollow noopener">

<div id="review_1153254886" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1153254886" />
    <a title="Iris P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2641948-iris-p"><img alt="Iris P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483406411p2/2641948.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1153254886?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Iris P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Iris P" href="/user/show/2641948-iris-p">Iris P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22909597-brown-girl-dreaming">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=audio-books">audio-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=african-american-history">african-american-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=critically-aclaimed">critically-aclaimed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=race-issues">race-issues</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2641948-iris-p?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1153254886" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14682264207483022489"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22909597.Brown_Girl_Dreaming' title='Brown Girl Dreaming by Jacqueline Woodson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Brown Girl Dreaming</a><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/74640.Jacqueline_Woodson' title='Jacqueline Woodson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jacqueline Woodson</a><br/><br/><br/><a href='http://s1341.photobucket.com/user/irisper01/media/woodson_jacqueline_p_lg_1_zpsbilbpwc8.jpg.html' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1432837573i/15010747.jpg' alt=' photo woodson_jacqueline_p_lg_1_zpsbilbpwc8.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><b>
  <i>The author won the National Book Award in 2014 in the category of Young People's Literature for Brown Girl Dreaming</i>
</b><br/><br/>We tend to believe that the way our memory process works is similar to a movie playing back in our heads in a sort of linear, chronological order, in reality though, at least in my experience, memories come in flashes and moments, not in long scenes like in a book or a documentary. <br/><br/>According to some literary critics, the use of poems as a</span>
  <span id="freeText14682264207483022489" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22909597.Brown_Girl_Dreaming" title="Brown Girl Dreaming by Jacqueline Woodson" rel="nofollow noopener">Brown Girl Dreaming</a><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/74640.Jacqueline_Woodson" title="Jacqueline Woodson" rel="nofollow noopener">Jacqueline Woodson</a><br><br><br><a href="http://s1341.photobucket.com/user/irisper01/media/woodson_jacqueline_p_lg_1_zpsbilbpwc8.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1432837573i/15010747.jpg" alt=" photo woodson_jacqueline_p_lg_1_zpsbilbpwc8.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>
  <i>The author won the National Book Award in 2014 in the category of Young People's Literature for Brown Girl Dreaming</i>
</b><br><br>We tend to believe that the way our memory process works is similar to a movie playing back in our heads in a sort of linear, chronological order, in reality though, at least in my experience, memories come in flashes and moments, not in long scenes like in a book or a documentary. <br><br>According to some literary critics, the use of poems as a format to write memoirs was pretty much unheard of before Woodson did it. And I have to say that it does work beautifully on the lyrical and mesmerizing "Brown Girl Dreaming".<br><br><b>
  <i>"Memory doesn't come as a straight narrative," Woodson said about why she didn’t choose prose. "It comes in small moments with all this white space."</i>
</b><br><br>With its innovative and creative use of succinct, vivid poems and vignettes "Brown Girl Dreaming" is a unique memoir that explores the power of dreams and words and how writing can help you make sense of the world you live in and in the process find your life's purpose.<br><br>On an interview with NPR, Woodson describes that the first time she discovered poetry and loved it was in elementary school when reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/36910.Langston_Hughes" title="Langston Hughes" rel="nofollow noopener">Langston Hughes.</a> <br><br><b>
  <i>"Until then, I thought it was some code that older white people used to speak to each other. I didn't know what was going on with the line breaks and the words," Woodson recalls. "Once the floodgates opened, they opened."</i>
</b><br><br>The world Woodson was born into, was turbulent and chaotic, and she skillfully brings the reader along to experience how it was to grow up in America as a black girl during the 1960's and 1970's . <br><br>Growing up during the last throes of the Jim Crow era and right in the midst of the Civil Rights Movement, Woodson weaves these poems to narrate both her family and personal stories as well as the political upheaval of the times.<br><br>In the background little Jacqueline can hear the grown-ups discussing civil rights marches, non-violence resistance training, MLK and Malcolm X, Freedom Singers and a girl named Ruby Bridges. Seeing these political events through the eyes of a child provides a wonderful and different perspective.<br><br>"Brown Girl Dreaming" starts on February 12, 1963 in Columbus, Ohio, the day Woodson was born in a "country caught between Black and White" as she describes it.<br><br>The poem "A Girl name Jack" is about her parents arguing to choose her name is amusing:<br><blockquote>
<b>A Girl Name Jack</b><br>Good enough name for me, my father said<br>the day I was born.<br>Don’t see why<br>she can’t have it, too.<br>But the women said no.<br>My mother first.<br>Then each aunt, pulling my pink blanket back<br>patting the crop of thick curls,<br>tugging at my new toes,<br>touching my cheeks.<br>We won’t have a girl named Jack, my mother said.</blockquote><br>As you can tell by the author's given name, you know who won that argument!<br><br><a href="http://s1341.photobucket.com/user/irisper01/media/Problem_web_zpsv4tscuie.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1432841279i/15011482._SX540_.jpg" alt="Ruby Bridges photo Problem_web_zpsv4tscuie.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>
  <i>The Problem We All Live With by Norman Rockwell depicts Ruby Bridges as a 6 year-old on her way to school during the process of racial desegregation in the South.</i>
</b><br><br>Her parent's differences didn't end with their little squabble about choosing Jacqueline's name. They were also divided by Jacqueline's mom yearning to go back to her native South Carolina and her dad's disdain of the South. <br><br>Here's a poem that describes their differences:<br><blockquote>
<b>Journey</b><br>You can keep your South, my father says.<br>The way they treated us down there,<br>I got your mama out as quick as I could.<br>Brought her right up here to Ohio.<br>Told here there's never gonna be a Woodson<br>that sits in the back of the bus.<br>Never gonna be a Woodson that has to <br>Yes sir and No sir white people.<br>Never gonna be a Woodson made to look down at the ground.<br>All your kids are stronger than that, my father says.<br>All you Woodson kids deserve to be<br>as good as you already are.<br>Yes sirree, Bob, my father says.<br>You can keep your South Carolina.</blockquote><br>This poem provides a window into how Woodson love for writing began:<br><blockquote>
<b>The Beginning</b><br>I cannot write a word yet but at three,<br>I now know that letter <i>J</i><br>love the way it curves into a hook<br>that I carefully top with a straight hat<br>the way my sister has taught me to do. <br>Love the sound of the letter and the promise<br>that one day this will be connected to a full name,<br>my own.<br>That I will be able to write<br>by myself.<br>Without my sister's hand over mine,<br>making it do what I cannot yet do.<br>How amazing these words are that slowly come to me.<br>How wonderfully on and on they go.<br>Will the words end, I ask<br>whenever I remember to.<br>Nope, my sister says, all of five years old now,<br>and promising me<br>infinity.</blockquote><br>I read this book as I listened along to Woodson's own narration of the audiobook version. The rhythm of the short poems makes for a great read-aloud and who better to perform her own words than the writer herself?<br><br>This book is both personal and political, is about family, friendship, religion, race and prejudice but at its core, is about the author's love of writing and how she discovered her passion for it.<br><br>That Woodson so eloquently crafted these linked-poems to create a narrative that flows so naturally, is a testament to her talents as a writer and to the power and beauty of the written word.<br><br>So Jacqueline with a J &amp; a Q, I salute you for writing such a wonderful book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14682264207483022489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Brown Girl Dreaming is a poetic account of Woodson's family life while at the same time giving a very good idea about what life was like growing up in the South and in New York.   <span id="freeText15126923728111759075" style="display:none">I was really excited to finally receive my copy of Brown Girl Dreaming this month. I as anticipating reading something by Jacqueline Woodson who I had herd so much about. Brown Girl Dreaming is a poetic account of Woodson's family life while at the same time giving a very good idea about what life was like growing up in the South and in New York. Beautifully written and telling a sensitive true story of how she felt about things. I enjoyed everything about it. I can see this book being used a pedagogical tool to encourage writing and to explore life for Blacks in the 60s and 70s. It's a wonderful read for all ages. It. Rings back good and bad memories but the closeness of family Is the universal theme that speaks to us all. You really should check it out. It would also make a great Christmas present - in the hardcover edition which really is very pretty.</span>

<div id="review_3092686622" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3092686622" />
    <a title="Reading_ Tam_ Ishly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/102159719-reading-tam-ishly"><img alt="Reading_ Tam_ Ishly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578580640p2/102159719.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3092686622?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Reading_ Tam_ Ishly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Reading_ Tam_ Ishly" href="/user/show/102159719-reading-tam-ishly">Reading_ Tam_ Ishly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3092686622&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3092686622" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10929211393716140243">I am so glad my favourite booktubers recommended this book again and again over the years 💕<br/><br/>*An important book on how the black community struggled for their own identity<br/><br/>***Written beautifully about family, relatives and how lives change when one has enough courage to live their dreams<br/><br/><br/>***Deals with racism, discrimination and violence against the black people <br/><br/>***Upbringing, religion and most importantly how it was like growing up in the 60s during the Civil Rights Movement<br/><br/>***The writing is really </span>
  <span id="freeText10929211393716140243" style="display:none">I am so glad my favourite booktubers recommended this book again and again over the years 💕<br /><br />*An important book on how the black community struggled for their own identity<br /><br />***Written beautifully about family, relatives and how lives change when one has enough courage to live their dreams<br /><br /><br />***Deals with racism, discrimination and violence against the black people <br /><br />***Upbringing, religion and most importantly how it was like growing up in the 60s during the Civil Rights Movement<br /><br />***The writing is really good<br /><br />***One of the best books written in verse<br /><br />*This book is important regarding so many things about the life of the author.<br />Apart from everything mentioned before, the writing also focuses on the difficulties the author faced because of her own learning issues and how it was like growing up with constant comparisons alongside her other 'smart' sibling.<br /><br />***I really like how the author described her siblings. I loved reading about her bookworm sister and her younger brother who's obsessed with comic books and superheroes. Yes, that got me. <br /><br />***There are some parts dealing with deaths and grief which I feel was handled well.<br />I cried too much when there's one part where the younger kid brother dies. It just broke my heart.<br /><br />First half of the book is filled with family vibes, all innocence and overflowing love of parents , grandparents and siblings. Yes, it also highlighted how the adults in the family thought about the unrest and the movement.<br /><br />The second half of the book developed into something really beautiful but also sad and wholesome.<br /><br />"I am not my sister."<br /><br />I really hate it when everyone keeps comparing someone with their so called 'smarter' siblings.<br /><br />I can totally relate to that! Parents, teachers, relatives, grandparents, neighbours. <br /><br />I say each one of us is smart in our own way. <br /><br />Guess everyone isn't smart enough to understand this.<br /><br />The whole book has five parts of which her writing really shines in part IV.<br /><br />My most favourite parts of the book are the last two parts in which the writing drastically changes and has more confidence. <br /><br />I love how the author never thought of giving up. She got hold of her dream. She believed in herself. And yes, she wrote the book I just finished up reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10929211393716140243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17377154996714495146">What a pleasant surprise this one was for me. I heard a lot about the story some time ago and added it to my list. Now, with Woodson's new book out, I've been hearing more about her and both of these books. I added this one to my audio queue and didn't think much when it arrived.<br/><br/>The story is the early life story of the author. When she was growing up, very young, splitting her time between the South and New York City. I grabbed the audio, which is read by the author. Immediately something seemed</span>
  <span id="freeText17377154996714495146" style="display:none">What a pleasant surprise this one was for me. I heard a lot about the story some time ago and added it to my list. Now, with Woodson's new book out, I've been hearing more about her and both of these books. I added this one to my audio queue and didn't think much when it arrived.<br /><br />The story is the early life story of the author. When she was growing up, very young, splitting her time between the South and New York City. I grabbed the audio, which is read by the author. Immediately something seemed a bit 'different' on the audio. I forgot that this book is a series of poems, almost a stream of thought at times. I wondered if perhaps I would loose something on this by not having the print, and listening to the audio. But I was so drawn to this story and Woodson does a wonderful job with the audio. <br /><br />I loved hearing the stories of her growing up, her siblings, her grandparents. I did not want this one to end. I still have the little song in my head that her Dad/Grandfather would sing, and that she would sing in the audio. I'm so glad that I finally picked this one up and that I listened via audio. I've already ordered Woodson's latest book, but sadly, she does not narrate this one.<br /><br />Overall, I would highly suggest this one, and grab the audio. You will not be disappointed.</span>

<div id="review_1697169753" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1697169753" />
    <a title="Cheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106692-cheri"><img alt="Cheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601402708p2/106692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1697169753?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1697169753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7562619887073491824"><br/>There is something so very real, honest, and about Jacqueline Woodson’s writing, regardless of what she’s writing about in “Brown Girl Dreaming.” Her prose contains heartrending stories, thoughts, musings, and emotions ranging from bliss to anger. There’s a childlike purity in her work, these snippets of thoughts that tell her story, stories… the story of her family, friends, her beliefs, her religion. <br/><br/>Growing up in the south in an era where so much change was taking place, where children were s</span>
  <span id="freeText7562619887073491824" style="display:none"><br />There is something so very real, honest, and about Jacqueline Woodson’s writing, regardless of what she’s writing about in “Brown Girl Dreaming.” Her prose contains heartrending stories, thoughts, musings, and emotions ranging from bliss to anger. There’s a childlike purity in her work, these snippets of thoughts that tell her story, stories… the story of her family, friends, her beliefs, her religion. <br /><br />Growing up in the south in an era where so much change was taking place, where children were surrounded from the outside with the message to be proud, and where the message from the older generation was still to avoid eye contact, you might expect more anger, more focus on the ugly side of that time. It’s not glossed over, it’s that the focus for those years shared in “Brown Girl Dreaming” is love for the place, the people and her memories. The nostalgia is sweet without sacrificing any truth, her power in the restraint she shows.<br /><br />“The first time I write my full name Jacqueline Amanda Woodson without anybody’s help on a clean white page in composition notebook, I know if I wanted to I could write anything. Letters becoming words, words gathering meaning, becoming thoughts outside my head becoming sentences written by Jacqueline Amanda Woodson.”<br /><br />This is the story of one girl finding her voice. <br /><br />This is also the story of a part of America’s racial history. <br /><br />This is Woodson’s story, but it’s also a story that is part of all of us. <br /><br />“The people who came before me worked so hard to make this world a better place for me. I know my work is to make the world a better place for those coming after. As long as I can remember this, I can continue to do the work I was put here to do.”<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7562619887073491824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1172908408" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1172908408" />
    <a title="Dianne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2947323-dianne"><img alt="Dianne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577914767p2/2947323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1172908408?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dianne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dianne" href="/user/show/2947323-dianne">Dianne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2947323-dianne?shelf=best-of-2015">best-of-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1172908408" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18224561457225858717">Really lovely. Woodson looks back at her childhood in a collection of free form "poems," in a stream of consciousness format. This may sound odd, but it is actually very astute. Don't we all remember our childhoods in bits and pieces; a mash-up of scattered events, snatches of conversations, impressions, feelings, scents and sounds? Woodson grows up partly in the south at a time of great social change, which makes this memoir all the more compelling. <br/><br/>A 4.5 for me. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1127582963" />
    <a title="Book Riot Community" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33447134-book-riot-community"><img alt="Book Riot Community" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583162430p2/33447134.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1127582963?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Book Riot Community" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Book Riot Community" href="/user/show/33447134-book-riot-community">Book Riot Community</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33447134-book-riot-community?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33447134-book-riot-community?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33447134-book-riot-community?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1127582963" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10751339354282244715">Last year I read Another Brooklyn and was bummed out that I couldn’t really get into it. The writing was amazing but the characters felt distant. Still, I had heard only good things about Brown Girl Dreaming, so I decided to give it a try. I’m glad I did! Whatever prevented me from connecting Another Brooklyn was clearly not present in Brown Girl Dreaming. The writing is elegantly simple, making it accessible to readers of every age. Woodson’s vignettes of her childhood growing up during the Civ</span>
  <span id="freeText10751339354282244715" style="display:none">Last year I read Another Brooklyn and was bummed out that I couldn’t really get into it. The writing was amazing but the characters felt distant. Still, I had heard only good things about Brown Girl Dreaming, so I decided to give it a try. I’m glad I did! Whatever prevented me from connecting Another Brooklyn was clearly not present in Brown Girl Dreaming. The writing is elegantly simple, making it accessible to readers of every age. Woodson’s vignettes of her childhood growing up during the Civil Rights Movement in New York and South Carolina are powerful and heartfelt. I highly recommend the audiobook, which is narrated by the author.<br /><br />— Kate Scott<br /><br /><br />from The Best Books We Read In April 2017: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/2017/05/01/riot-round-best-books-read-april-4/">http://bookriot.com/2017/05/01/riot-r...</a> <br />____________________<br /><br /><br />This book is getting a lot of attention recently due to some less than favorable situations that occurred at the National Book Awards. However, the one plus side to that has been the fact that so many more people have been exposed to Brown Girl Dreaming. I was not prepared at all for what this book was. I went into it with slight hesitation. I don’t read a lot of middle grade books. I am not extremely comfortable with books written in verse. But everything about this book was perfection. Jacqueline Woodson’s experiences are so real and honest and provide such insight into the life of a young black girl growing up in the South in the 1960s. I have been shoving this book into people’s hands since I’ve read it and I can see myself continuing to do so for a long, long time. -Rincey Abraham<br /><br />From Best Books of 2014: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/2014/12/02/riot-round-best-books-2014/">http://bookriot.com/2014/12/02/riot-r...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10751339354282244715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
